Drivers of B2B Software Purchase Decisions\n\nAuthors\n\nClaudia Loebbecke, Thomas Weiss, Philip Powell, Christin Krooss Pages\n\n535 - 546\n\nDOI\n\n10.3233/978-1-60750-577-8-535\n\nSeries Frontiers in Artificial Intelligence and Applications\n\nEbook Volume 212: Bridging the Socio-technical Gap in Decision Support Systems\n\nAbstract Organizations are increasingly turning to large, enterprise-wide software systems as potential solutions to managing complex business-to-business (B2B) relationships. These systems become so embedded into the business that they are best understood as socio-technical. The procurement process for such systems needs to be better understood. The purchase decision is seen as being based upon information-related and feature-related drivers. This paper develops a model of software purchase decisions and investigates seven resulting hypotheses. Testing with a sample of customers from a major, global software supplier demonstrates that information-related drivers, customer references and expert network recommendations, and feature-related drivers, price performance, functionality, and sales team service, play a major role in B2B software purchase decisions.\n\n---\n\nTitle: An empirical study on the influences on the acquisition of enterprise ...\nURL: https://ideas.repec.org/p/hal/journl/hal-03218579.html\nPublished: N/A\nAuthor: Ramaraj Palanisamy & Jacques Verville & Christine Bernadas & Nazim Taskin\nHighlights:\nThe purpose of this paper is to understand the decision process of enterprise software acquisition. The research aims to focus on identifying significant influences on enterprise software acquisition decisions. As a research model and theoretical background, the organizational buying model (OBB) is proposed for the acquisition of enterprise systems. Influences on enterprise software acquisition decision processes were found by an empirical study carried out from a practitioner's perspective. The study collected data via a mail survey administered to information systems (IS) professionals involved in the acquisition of enterprise software (ES). The survey questionnaire was developed based on a previous research project and a literature review. Organizational buying behavior (OBB) models in the literature served as the basis for the influences included in the survey instrument. Factor analysis was carried out on the survey data to identify the most significant factors/influences. The following five factors emerged as significant influences on the acquisition decision process of enterprise software: ES strategy and performance; BPR and adaptability; management commitment and user buy‐in; single vendor integrated solution; and consultants, team‐location, and vendor's  ...    study findings and guidelines for further research are suggested. The present study provides a starting point for further research in understanding a more comprehensive list of influences on enterprise software acquisition. A bigger sample from more industries is required to examine whether the significance of the influences remains stable. PurposeThe purpose of this paper is to understand the decision process of enterprise software acquisition. The research aims to focus on identifying significant influences on enterprise software acquisition decisions. Design/methodology/approachAs a research model and theoretical background, the organizational buying model (OBB) is proposed for the acquisition of enterprise systems. Influences on enterprise software acquisition decision processes were found by an empirical study carried out from a practitioner's perspective. The study collected data via a mail survey administered to information systems (IS) professionals involved in the acquisition of enterprise software (ES). The survey questionnaire was developed based on a previous research project and a literature review. Organizational buying behavior (OBB) models in the literature served as the basis for the influences included in the survey instrument. Factor analysis was carried out on the survey data to identify the most significant factors/influences. FindingsThe following five factors emerged as significant influences on the acquisition decision process of enterprise software: ES strategy and performance; BPR and adaptability; management commitment and user buy‐in; single vendor integrated solution; and consultants, team‐location, and vendor's financing. These factors are discussed and managerial  ...    suggested. Research limitations/implicationsThe present study provides a starting point for further research in understanding a more comprehensive list of influences on enterprise software acquisition. A bigger sample from more industries is required to examine whether the significance of the influences remains stable. Originality/valueUsing OBB models has proven to be useful for organizations in making effective decisions on enterprise software acquisition.  ...    16. De Boer, L., Holmen, E. and Sitar, C.P. The software acquisition problem is depicted as two dimensional: what acquisition approach to use (custom develop or base it on a package) and who should complete the task (internal resources or external providers). Although there are a lot of studies about aspects of the sourcing decision, the custom/package decision has previously received little attention in the literature. However, decision on whether to develop software applications or buy commercially available products is a common dilemma that frequently emerges. This “build or buy ” decision has become even more complex in recent years with new developments in the enterprise software environment. To make the best choice, firms must weigh the top considerations of their business processes against the advantages of each type of system. In this paper, we introduce a framework to support “build or buy ” decision in the enterprise software acquisition process.  ...   ... Read more\n\n## Key takeawaysAI 1. Organizations must select alternatives meeting an acceptable functional coverage level or resort to custom development. 2. The heuristic algorithm quantitatively assesses software based on assigned weights and performance values. 3. A project team must define requirements through business process reengineering (BPR) for effective software selection. 4. Functional suitability scores, ranging from 0 to 1, evaluate software alternatives against system requirements. 5. The proposed framework aids firms in making informed 'build or buy' decisions for enterprise software.  ...    1. {"parentUuid":"0e9cc477-ad24-421b-9d73-431ba3779cc4","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"c0b837de-1474-4940-aea8-21bf4f002cd9","agentId":"a209a6f8f4e79c522","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01XJgLf5M6EX7zxekEYggrnf","type":"tool_result","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Detailed Results:\n\nTitle: Crossing the Chasm, 3rd Edition: The Updated Version of ...\nURL: https://www.amazon.com/Crossing-Chasm-3rd-Disruptive-Mainstream/dp/0062292986\nContent: In Crossing the Chasm, Geoffrey A. Moore shows that in the Technology Adoption Life Cycle - which begins with innovators and moves to early adopters, early majority, late majority, and laggards - there is a vast chasm between the early adopters and the early majority. While early adopters are willing to sacrifice for the advantage of being first, the early majority waits until they know that the technology actually offers improvements in productivity. The challenge for innovators and marketers is to narrow this chasm and ultimately accelerate adoption across every segment. [...] Full content visible, double tap to read brief content.\n\nGeoffrey Moore is an author, speaker, and advisor who splits his consulting time between start-up companies in the Wildcat Venture Partners portfolios and established high-tech enterprises, most recently including Salesforce, Microsoft, Autodesk, F5Networks, Gainsight, Google, and Splunk. [...] This third edition brings Moore's classic work up to date with dozens of new examples of successes and failures, new strategies for marketing in the digital world, and Moore's most current insights and findings. He also includes two new appendices, the first connecting the ideas in Crossing the Chasm to work subsequently published in his Inside the Tornado, and the second presenting his recent groundbreaking work for technology adoption models for high-tech consumer markets.\n\nTitle: [PDF] Crossing the Chasm: From Adoption to Diffusion of a Telehealth ...\nURL: https://scispace.com/pdf/crossing-the-chasm-from-adoption-to-diffusion-of-a-b5ql1msnkz.pdf\nContent: Anchoring our theoretical framing on the process-oriented approach to diffusion of a complex, networked technology, we draw on, in particular, Moore’s (1999, 2004) concept of chasm for the analysis of the study. Moore describes the common delay that accompanies diffusion of an innovation, following an initial period of rapid uptake. He describes this plateau in the diffusion process as a chasm that needs to be crossed—from the early adopter cohort to the much larger “early majority” in the technology adoption life cycle. His focus on identifying this chasm is to explain why many innovations fail to achieve more large-scale diffusion (i.e., the early majority), after being adopted enthu-siastically by early adopters. We borrow the chasm metaphor to describe the problematic gap that can [...] However, many telehealth initiatives fail to go beyond the status of prototype applications despite being considered technically viable and medically rele-vant. Based on a longitudinal investigation of a successful telehealth program, we identify a chasm between the initial adoption mode of the innovation as a prototype within a network of hospitals and the subsequent diffusion mode of the innovation as a commercialized product. Subsequently, we analyze how key actors negotiated the chasm to successfully diffuse the innovation beyond the initial hospital setting. In terms of research, the paper presents a longi-tudinal, empirical investigation of a successful telehealth innovation. Drawing on the metaphor of “crossing the chasm,” we explain why many telehealth initiatives fail to go [...] Fichman and Kemerer (1999) identify an assimilation gap between acquisition of an innovation and its actual deployment and use in an organization; and Moore (1999, 2004) uses the phrase “crossing the chasm” to denote a gap between early adopters and the early majority of innovation adopters in the marketplace. In framing this research, we rely on this notion of a gap in the process of market uptake of an innovation; however, the gap we investigate has a different focus and is framed differently than in previous studies. Specifically, we draw on Moore’s concept of chasm to analyze the disrupted path to commercialization and diffusion of a telehealth innovation.\n\nTitle: Geoffrey Moore on the technology adoption lifecycle\nURL: https://www.driverlesscrocodile.com/books-and-recommendations/crossing-the-chasm-geoffrey-moore-on-the-technology-adoption-lifecycle/\nContent: Skip to content \n\n Home\n What is Driverless Croc?\n Who are you?\n Greatest Hits\n HamsterTach\n JakartaLives\n Moneyfesto\n\n# Crossing the Chasm: Geoffrey Moore on the technology adoption lifecycle\n\n by Stuart Patience\n\nThis is the best summary presentation on Crossing the Chasm that I’ve seen, from Moore himself.\n\nHe discusses the diffusion of “technology products” (i.e. things to do with computers), but the ideas are highly relevant to marketing any kind of solution to a problem, from plumbing services to public health and literacy, whether aimed at individual consumers or government departments and large businesses.\n\nHighly recommended. This process can be thought of as a continuum with definable stages, each associated with a definable group, and each group making up a predictable portion of the whole. [...] Whereas other industries introduce discontinuous innova­ tions only occasionally and with much trepidation, high-tech enterprises do so routinely and as confidently as a born-again Christian holding four aces. From their inception, there­ fore, high-tech industries have needed a marketing model that coped effectively with this type of product introduction. Thus the Technology Adoption Life Cycle became central to the entire sector’s approach to marketing. (People are usually amused to learn that the original research that gave rise to this model was done on the adoption of new strains of seed pota­ toes among American fanners. Despite these agrarian roots, however, the model has thoroughly transplanted itself into the soil of Silicon Valley.) The model describes the market penetration of [...] Valley.) The model describes the market penetration of any new technology product in terms of a progression in the types of consumers it attracts throughout its useful life: High-Tech Marketing Illusion 15 TECHNOLOGY ADOPTION LIFE CYCLE As you can see, we have a bell curve. The divisions in the curve are roughly equivalent to where standard deviations would fall. That is, the early majority and the late majority fall within one standard deviation of the mean, the early adopters and the laggards within two, and way out there, at the very onset of a new technology, about three standard deviations from the norm, are the innovators.\n\nTitle: Crossing the chasm: Understanding users' motivational differences ...\nURL: https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0378720624001320\nContent: ### Int. [...] According to Moore , an innovation adoption chasm marks the failure of a high-technology product to cross over to the mainstream market after successful early sales. The chasm results from a fundamental motivational difference between the purchase behavior of early market customers and that of mainstream customers. Early customers are more likely to be risk-takers, willing to pay to be first to try new products, while mainstream market customers purchase the product only once it has been shown to work well. Although chasm theory was developed in the context of high-technology adoption, it also may be applied to the online community context. Iriberri and Leroy  suggested that the development of an online community also evolves in stages, which may promote stage-dependent differences in [...] Another stage-related threat for online communities is the chasm problem, which describes changes in the relationship between users’ motivation and their participation behavior as an online community develops. A failure to transition from early adoption to later adoption might be observed if an online community maintains the same strategy throughout different stages without observing or incorporating shifts in user needs and expectations. Such motivational changes have been observed extensively in the context of high-technology adoption behavior ; however, whether the same phenomenon exists in online communities remains unknown. The literature has explored the diverse effects of user motivation across each stage of a product's life cycle by introducing time-of-adoption as a moderator .\n\nTitle: [PDF] Crossing the Chasm : Marketing and Selling High-tech Products to ...\nURL: http://soloway.pbworks.com/w/file/fetch/46715502/Crossing-The-Chasm.pdf\nContent: Whereas other industries introduce discontinuous innovations only occa-sionally and with much trepidation, high-tech enterprises do so routinely and as confidently as a born-again Christian holding four aces. [...] Although we all experienced our fates uniquely, much of our shared expe-rience can be summarized by recasting the Technology Adoption Life Cycle in the following way: As you can see, the components of the life cycle are unchanged, but between any two psychographic groups has been introduced a gap. This sym-bolizes the dissociation between the two groups—that is, the difficulty any group will have in accepting a new product if it is presented in the same way as it was to the group to its immediate left. Each of these gaps represents an opportunity for marketing to lose momentum, to miss the transition to the next segment, thereby never to gain the promised land of profit-margin lead-ership in the middle of the bell curve.\n\nTitle: Crossing The AI Chasm With Technology Thinker And ...\nURL: https://www.forbes.com/sites/randybean/2024/07/14/crossing-the-ai-chasm-with-technology-thinker-and-visionary-geoffrey-moore/\nContent: Moore has spoken about the disruptive potential of AI in “white collar work that requires creative intelligence”. {"parentUuid":"3f1f7f4c-c87e-4752-baa8-955e54ab244e","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a209a6f8f4e79c522","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_017rmRZrqjg12TADVyxUxnG4","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Here is the complete research compilation. All findings below come from the web searches and scrapes performed during this session -- no training data.\n\n---\n\n# Academic Research: Win/Loss Analysis, Competitive Deal Analysis & B2B Buying Decisions\n\n## COMPLETE CITATION INDEX WITH KEY FINDINGS\n\n---\n\n## 1. FOUNDATIONAL MODELS OF ORGANIZATIONAL BUYING BEHAVIOR\n\n### 1a. Webster, F.E. Jr. & Wind, Y. (1972). \"A General Model for Understanding Organizational Buying Behavior\"\n- **Journal**: Journal of Marketing, 36(2), 12-19\n- **DOI**: 10.1177/002224297203600204\n- **Cited by**: 1,810+ times\n- **Full PDF**: https://faculty.wharton.upenn.edu/wp-content/uploads/2012/04/7215_A_General_Model_for_Understanding.pdf\n- **Key Findings**: Proposes four classes of variables determining organizational buying: (1) environmental, (2) organizational, (3) interpersonal (buying center dynamics), and (4) individual factors. Established that organizational buying is a decision-making process carried out by individuals, in interaction with other people, within the context of a formal organization, influenced by environmental forces. The model remains the most-cited framework in OBB research 50+ years later.\n\n### 1b. Robinson, P.J., Faris, C.W. & Wind, Y. (1967). \"Industrial Buying and Creative Marketing\"\n- **Publisher**: Allyn & Bacon, Boston\n- **BuyGrid 25-Year Retrospective**: https://faculty.wharton.upenn.edu/wp-content/uploads/2012/04/9802_The_BuyGrid_Model_30_Years.pdf\n- **Key Findings**: Introduces the **BuyGrid framework** -- classifies buying situations into three types (new task, modified rebuy, straight rebuy) and maps them against buying process phases (\"buyphases\"). Research supports validity of its basic dimensions: buying situation, buying process, and buying center. Still appears in major marketing textbooks (Kotler 1994, Haas 1992). Wind & Thomas (1996) retrospective found the model stimulated 25+ years of research and remains a coherent organizing framework.\n\n### 1c. Sheth, J.N. (1973). \"A Model of Industrial Buyer Behavior\"\n- **Journal**: Journal of Marketing, 37(4), 50-56\n- **DOI**: 10.1177/002224297303700408\n- **Key Findings**: Integrates existing knowledge into a descriptive model focusing on joint decision-making and psychological aspects of buyers. Identifies key elements impacting buying: background and roles of individuals, information sources consulted, expectations of stakeholders, perceptual distortions, types of purchases, and supplier/brand choice factors. Includes conflict resolution approaches. Has been extended by later researchers to include manufacturing flexibility, long-term relationships, performance, and trust.\n\n### 1d. Johnston, W.J. & Lewin, J.E. (1996). \"Organizational Buying Behavior: Toward an Integrative Framework\"\n- **Journal**: Journal of Business Research, 35(1), 1-15\n- **DOI**: 10.1016/0148-2963(94)00077-8\n- **URL**: https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/0148296394000778\n- **Key Findings**: Meta-analysis/review synthesizing 25+ years of OBB research post-BuyGrid. Analyzed the cumulative body of research following the seminal works of Robinson, Faris, and Wind (1967) and Webster and Wind (1972). Provided an integrative framework organizing the fragmented research landscape.\n\n### 1e. Lilien, G.L. et al. (2015). \"Business-to-Business Buying: Challenges and Opportunities\"\n- **Journal**: Customer Needs and Solutions, 2, 193-208\n- **DOI**: 10.1007/s40547-015-0040-5\n- **Key Findings**: Identified four key aspects through a Delphi-like process via the Institute for the Study of Business Markets (ISBM): (1) the changing landscape of B2B buying, (2) increasing sophistication of sellers, (3) impact of technological changes, and (4) the need for new research approaches. Notes that B2B transactions represent the majority of US commerce.\n\n---\n\n## 2. VENDOR SELECTION CRITERIA RESEARCH\n\n### 2a. Dickson, G.W. (1966). \"An Analysis of Vendor Selection Systems and Decisions\"\n- **Journal**: Journal of Purchasing, 2(1), 5-17\n- **DOI**: 10.1111/j.1745-493X.1966.tb00818.x\n- **Cited by**: 1,782+ times (per scite.ai)\n- **Key Findings**: The foundational empirical study on vendor selection. Ranked **23 criteria** used by purchasing agents and managers. Top criteria: quality, delivery, performance history, warranties/claims policies, production facilities/capacity, price, technical capability, financial position. This ranking has been validated and extended for 60 years.\n\n### 2b. Weber, C.A., Current, J.R. & Benton, W.C. (1991). \"Vendor Selection Criteria and Methods\"\n- **Journal**: European Journal of Operational Research, 50(1), 2-18\n- **Key Findings**: Comprehensive review of **74 articles** published from 1966 to 1991 on vendor selection criteria and methods. Categorized criteria based on Dickson's 23 original criteria. Discussed how Just-In-Time manufacturing strategies impacted vendor selection criteria, showing a shift toward quality and delivery over price. Remains the standard literature review in vendor selection research.\n\n### 2c. De Boer, L., Labro, E. & Morlacchi, P. (2001). \"A Review of Methods Supporting Supplier Selection\"\n- **Journal**: European Journal of Purchasing & Supply Management, 7(2), 75-89\n- **Key Findings**: Updated review of supplier selection methods, categorizing decision-support approaches from simple weighted scoring to complex mathematical programming models.\n\n### 2d. Ho, W., Xu, X. & Dey, P.K. (2010). \"Multi-criteria Decision Making Approaches for Supplier Evaluation and Selection: A Literature Review\"\n- **Journal**: European Journal of Operational Research, 202(1), 16-24\n- **Key Findings**: Literature review finding that the most popular individual approach is DEA (Data Envelopment Analysis), followed by mathematical programming, AHP, and ANP. Integrated approaches combining AHP with goal programming were most common.\n\n---\n\n## 3. THE CHALLENGER SALE & B2B BUYING BEHAVIOR (CEB/GARTNER RESEARCH)\n\n### 3a. Dixon, M. & Adamson, B. (2011). \"The Challenger Sale: Taking Control of the Customer Conversation\"\n- **Publisher**: Penguin (Wall Street Journal bestseller)\n- **Research basis**: Survey of **6,000+ sales reps across 90 companies** and multiple geographies\n- **Key Findings**: Identified five distinct sales profiles: Hard Worker, Problem Solver, Challenger, Relationship Builder, Lone Wolf. Only the **Challenger** profile consistently outperforms, especially in complex B2B sales. Challenger reps teach customers something new about their business, tailor messages to stakeholders, and take control of the sale.\n\n### 3b. Adamson, B., Dixon, M. & Toman, N. (2012). \"The End of Solution Sales\"\n- **Journal**: Harvard Business Review, July-August 2012 (Reprint R1207C)\n- **URL**: https://hbr.org/2012/07/the-end-of-solution-sales\n- **Key Findings**: Argues that the traditional solution-selling model is obsolete because B2B customers now define their own solutions before engaging sellers. Companies can readily define their own problems and force suppliers into price-driven bake-offs. **\"Adaptive reps, who seek out customers primed for change, challenge them with provocative insights, and coach them on how to buy, will become indispensable.\"** Star performers don't just diagnose needs -- they teach, tailor, and take control.\n\n### 3c. CEB/Gartner B2B Buying Studies (2008-2019, ongoing)\n- **Source**: Challenger Inc. (spun off from CEB/Gartner 2018)\n- **URL**: https://challengerinc.com/decade-research-how-b2b-buyers-make-purchase-decisions/\n- **Key Findings from multiple waves**:\n  - **57%** of the purchase process is completed before buyers engage a sales rep\n  - **53%** of B2B buyers say the **sales experience** (not brand, product, service, or price) is the #1 driver of purchase loyalty\n  - Buying group size grew from **5.4 stakeholders (2014)** to **6.8 (2016)**, with each stakeholder bringing competing objectives\n  - **39%** of customers report being overwhelmed by the buying process\n  - **89%** of buyers identify business problems without supplier input\n  - **61%** of B2B buyers prefer a rep-free buying experience (2024 Gartner survey of 632 buyers)\n  - **73%** of B2B buyers actively avoid suppliers who send irrelevant outreach\n  - **69%** report inconsistencies between website information and what sellers tell them\n\n---\n\n## 4. COMPETITIVE INTELLIGENCE EFFECTIVENESS RESEARCH\n\n### 4a. Ahearne, M., Lam, S.K., Hayati, B. & Kraus, F. (2013). \"Intrafunctional Competitive Intelligence and Sales Performance: A Social Network Perspective\"\n- **Journal**: Journal of Marketing, 77(5), 37-56\n- **DOI**: 10.1509/jm.11.0217\n- **Impact Factor**: 11.5\n- **Key Findings**: The most rigorous academic study on CI and sales performance. Using multilevel datasets from **two U.S. corporations**, found that salesperson CI quality positively impacts sales performance at both individual and district levels. However, **district CI quality diversity weakens this positive effect** -- meaning when CI quality varies across a sales district, performance suffers. Sales managers' network centrality buffers these losses, but individual salespeople's centrality does not. Highlights the role of managers as CI hubs.\n\n### 4b. Dzreke, S.S. & Dzreke, S.E. (2025). \"From Intelligence to Advantage: How Competitive Analysis Drives Profitability in the Digital Age\"\n- **Journal**: IJRAR (International Journal of Research and Analytical Reviews)\n- **URL**: https://www.ijrar.org/papers/IJRAR25B3416.pdf\n- **Key Findings**: Analysis of **200 enterprises** reveals organizations adopting AI-enhanced competitive intelligence achieve **18-22% higher profitability** than peers. However, **25% of firms experience diminished strategic judgment** from over-reliance on data -- a phenomenon termed **\"algorithmic myopia.\"** Technology and healthcare firms lead in AI-driven CI adoption.\n\n### 4c. Crayon State of Competitive Intelligence Reports (2018-2025)\n- **Source**: Crayon (survey of 1,000+ CI practitioners, largest study of its kind)\n- **URL**: https://www.crayon.co/state-of-competitive-intelligence\n- **Key Findings**:\n  - **61%** of businesses say CI has made a direct impact on revenue (up from 52% the prior year)\n  - **71%** of businesses using competitive battlecards say they've improved win rates\n  - **72%** of businesses sharing CI weekly report direct revenue impact\n  - Sellers go head-to-head with competitors in **68%** of deals\n  - Average team rates itself only **3.8 out of 10** in competitive selling\n  - This gap costs companies **$2-10 million per year** in lost deals\n  - Companies using conversational intelligence tools (Gong, Chorus) for compete are increasing sales effectiveness by **82%**\n  - **76% year-over-year increase** in AI adoption for CI; 60% now use AI daily\n\n### 4d. Info-Tech Research Group (2026). \"Build Competitive Intelligence for Market Success\"\n- **Source**: Info-Tech Research Group blueprint\n- **URL**: https://www.prnewswire.com/news-releases/sales-teams-lose-competitive-deals-without-disciplined-competitive-intelligence-according-to-info-tech-research-group-302686626.html\n- **Key Findings**: CI programs deliver most value when designed to support sellers in live competitive situations and measured by CI-influenced wins. When CI is disconnected from sales workflows, insights fail to change seller behavior. Identifies systemic challenges: lack of accountability linking CI to win rates, functional silos, and stale data.\n\n---\n\n## 5. WIN/LOSS ANALYSIS RESEARCH\n\n### 5a. Gartner Research on Win/Loss Analysis\n- **Citation**: Gartner, \"Tech Go-to-Market: Three Ways Marketers Can Use Data From Win/Loss Analysis to Increase Win Rates and Revenues\"\n- **Key Finding**: Companies taking a comprehensive approach to win/loss analysis have seen **15-30% increases in revenue** and **up to 50% improvement in win rates**.\n\n### 5b. Corporate Visions Win/Loss Research\n- **Sample**: 100,000+ B2B purchase decisions across 500 companies and 50 industries\n- **URL**: https://corporatevisions.com/blog/win-loss-analysis-best-practices/\n- **Key Findings**: Sales teams are incorrect about deal outcomes **more than 60% of the time**. Internal CRM data about lost deals is **85% fundamentally wrong**. Third-party win/loss providers achieve better results -- companies using external providers are **38% more likely** to achieve win rate improvements of 10%+.\n\n### 5c. Klue 2025 Win-Loss Trends Report\n- **Sample**: 300+ marketing, product marketing, and CI leaders\n- **URL**: https://klue.com/win-loss-trends-report\n- **Key Findings**: \n  - **63%** of companies report win-rate increases from win/loss analysis; rises to **84%** for programs established 3+ years\n  - On average, **40%** of deals go through win/loss analysis\n  - Leaders using external research as a data source are **12% more likely** to report significant impact\n  - Leaders combining win/loss and CI programs are **nearly 3x more likely** to report significant/transformational impact\n  - **12%** of fully integrated programs report transformational impact vs. **2%** of non-integrated programs\n  - **41%** currently use AI in win/loss programs; another 41% plan to\n\n### 5d. Clozd/Pragmatic Institute State of Win-Loss Reports (2023, 2025)\n- **Key Findings**: Companies implementing comprehensive external win/loss programs achieve **15-30% revenue increases** and up to **50% win rate improvements**. **44%** of companies now outsource some win/loss analysis.\n\n### 5e. Anova Consulting Group (2011). \"Win/Loss Analysis\" (First book on the topic)\n- **Publisher**: McGraw-Hill\n- **Key Context**: In 2011 only ~20% of companies had formal win/loss practices. By 2025, estimated at ~50%.\n\n---\n\n## 6. PROSPECT THEORY & LOSS AVERSION IN B2B PURCHASING\n\n### 6a. Kahneman, D. & Tversky, A. (1979). \"Prospect Theory: An Analysis of Decision under Risk\"\n- **Journal**: Econometrica, 47(2), 263-292\n- **Full PDF**: https://web.mit.edu/curhan/www/docs/Articles/15341_Readings/Behavioral_Decision_Theory/Kahneman_Tversky_1979_Prospect_theory.pdf\n- **Key Findings**: People value losses approximately **twice as intensely** as equivalent gains. Decisions are evaluated relative to a reference point (status quo), not absolute values. People are risk-averse for gains but risk-seeking to avoid losses. The certainty effect: people prefer certain outcomes over merely probable ones.\n\n### 6b. Tversky, A. & Kahneman, D. (1992). \"Advances in Prospect Theory: Cumulative Representation of Uncertainty\"\n- **Journal**: Journal of Risk and Uncertainty, 5, 297-323\n- **Full PDF**: https://psych.fullerton.edu/mbirnbaum/psych466/articles/Tversky_Kahneman_JRU_92.pdf\n- **Key Findings**: Extended the original theory with cumulative prospect theory. Confirmed that **\"losses loom larger than gains\"** across risk and uncertainty contexts.\n\n### 6c. Application to B2B Sales: Challenger Inc. (2024). \"How Prospect Theory Drives Sales Experiences\"\n- **URL**: https://challengerinc.com/blog/prospect-theory-drives-sales-experiences/\n- **Key Findings**: Loss aversion directly explains **status quo bias** in B2B buying -- decision-makers stick with known vendors even when better solutions exist. The pain of potential loss (switching costs, risk, disruption) is perceived as greater than potential improvement. This explains why many deals end in **\"no decision\"** rather than competitive loss. The Challenger approach counters this by emphasizing the hidden costs of inaction rather than gains from switching.\n\n### 6d. De Boer, L. et al. (2008). \"Supplier Switching Decisions\"\n- **Journal**: European Journal of Operational Research (published online)\n- **DOI**: 10.1016/S0377-2217(06)01031-9\n- **Key Findings**: Even economically rational buyers demonstrate **considerable inertness** in supplier switching when incumbent cost structures are unknown. Status quo effect (Samuelson & Zeckhauser), norm theory, and decision avoidance all retard switching. Switching costs arise from prior commitments: specific physical, informational, artificially created, or psychological investments.\n\n### 6e. Baxter, R. et al. (2015). \"Securing Business-to-Business Relationships: The Impact of Switching Costs\"\n- **Journal**: Industrial Marketing Management, 52\n- **DOI**: 10.1016/j.indmarman.2015.05.010\n- **Key Findings**: Switching costs in B2B are multi-faceted: (i) procedural, (ii) financial, and (iii) relational. **Relational switching costs are most important** for securing B2B relationships -- they impact share-of-wallet, cross-buying behavior, and actual switching behavior. Procedural switching costs only influence share-of-wallet; financial switching costs solely impact cross-buying.\n\n---\n\n## 7. HEALTHCARE IT PROCUREMENT RESEARCH\n\n### 7a. Lammers, E.J. & Zheng, K. (2011). \"Characteristics Associated with Hospital Health IT Vendor Switching and Dropping\"\n- **Journal**: AMIA Annual Symposium Proceedings (PMC3243192)\n- **URL**: https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC3243192/\n- **Key Findings**: First study of vendor switching/dropping in hospital health IT. Found **34% of hospitals switched EMR/CPOE vendors** between 2003-2008. ~20% dropped systems entirely, indicating implementation challenges. Switching predictors: nonprofit ownership, larger hospitals, teaching hospitals, system membership. Barriers: financial constraints (high Medicaid share reduces switching), lack of IT resources.\n\n### 7b. Menachemi, N. et al. (2013). \"Health Information Technology Vendor Selection Strategies and Total Factor Productivity\"\n- **Journal**: Health Care Management Review, 38(3), 177-187\n- **DOI**: 10.1097/HMR.0b013e3182572c7b\n- **Affiliation**: Johns Hopkins University\n- **Key Findings**: Compared three HIT vendor selection strategies (best-of-breed, best-of-suite, single vendor). The **vendor selection strategy impacts technological change** required of an organization but **does not appear to positively or adversely impact technical efficiency or total factor productivity**. Higher technological change from best-of-breed/suite strategies may have more direct early impact but gains don't translate to long-term efficiency advantages.\n\n### 7c. Priestman, W. et al. (2019). \"Lessons Learned from a Comprehensive Electronic Patient Record Procurement Process\"\n- **Journal**: BMJ Health & Care Informatics, 26(1), e000020\n- **URL**: https://informatics.bmj.com/content/bmjhci/26/1/e000020.full.pdf\n- **Key Findings**: Case study from Great Ormond Street Hospital (NHS). Despite extensive scoring processes, self-reported vendor specs provided **relatively poor discrimination** between suppliers. **Demonstrations and site visits provided superior discriminatory value**. Key lessons: detailed organizational requirements preparation is critical; early staff involvement creates ownership; procurement advisors add significant value; the process must be customized to organizational needs.\n\n### 7d. PACS Selection Case Study (2025). \"A Comprehensive Guide to Selecting and Replacing PACS\"\n- **Journal**: Journal of Imaging Informatics in Medicine (Springer)\n- **DOI**: 10.1007/s10278-025-01672-7\n- **Key Findings**: Developed a six-phase process: team formation, expectation setting, surveys, standardized demonstrations, detailed RFP. Defined **11 pillars and 236 evaluation concepts** weighted across dimensions including usability, integration, performance, and cost. Five vendors shortlisted; onsite assessments used department-generated anonymized datasets. Ultimately the incumbent vendor was selected, reinforcing status quo bias.\n\n### 7e. Bal, C.G. & Ada, S. (2013). \"ICT Supplier Evaluation Criteria in Large University Hospitals\"\n- **Source**: Handbook of Research on ICTs and Management Systems for Improving Efficiency in Healthcare (IGI Global)\n- **DOI**: 10.4018/978-1-4666-3990-4.ch063\n- **Key Findings**: Surveyed 48 executives across 26 university hospitals in Turkey. Key criteria: physical characteristics, cost, efficiency, speed, security, productivity, advanced technology, technical service/warranty, capable personnel, in-service training. Found **significant differences** between medical and administrative executives in how they evaluate IT suppliers.\n\n### 7f. Scientific Reports (2023). \"Vendor Evaluation Platform for Medical Equipment Based on Multi-Criteria Decision-Making\"\n- **Journal**: Scientific Reports (Nature), 13\n- **DOI**: 10.1038/s41598-023-38902-3\n- **Key Findings**: Used ECRI standards for benchmarking. Top-weighted criteria: product performance/quality (~23%), cost/TCO (~23%), compliance/regulatory (~16%), service/support (~11%). Applied MOORA, SAW, and TOPSIS methods for vendor ranking.\n\n### 7g. Rinne, S.T. et al. (2023). \"Practices Supporting Electronic Health Record Transitions: Lessons from Four US Healthcare Systems\"\n- **Journal**: Journal of General Internal Medicine, 38, 1015-1022\n- **DOI**: 10.1007/s11606-023-08279-0\n- **Key Findings**: Multi-site qualitative study of EHR transitions (both Epic and Cerner). Pre-go-live practices: communicate rationale, understand baseline workflows, plan customization. Go-live: personalize training, invest in internal support, reduce workload expectations. Post-go-live: continuous optimization. Notes that Epic (32.9%) and Cerner (24.4%) hold largest US hospital EHR market shares.\n\n### 7h. Factors Influencing Procurement of Digital Healthcare (2021)\n- **Journal**: International Journal of Health Policy and Management, 11(9), 1883-1893\n- **DOI**: 10.34172/ijhpm.2021.115\n- **PMC**: PMC9808215\n- **Key Findings**: Dutch district nursing case study. Five procurement themes: rationale, provider-payer relationship, resources, evidence, and payment model. Fee-for-service payment models create barriers to digital health procurement. Low trust between purchasers and providers, combined with risk-aversion and short-term cost focus, inhibits innovation adoption.\n\n---\n\n## 8. ENTERPRISE SOFTWARE BUYING BEHAVIOR\n\n### 8a. Verville, J. & Halingten, A. (2003). \"A Six-Stage Model of the Buying Process for ERP Software\"\n- **Journal**: Industrial Marketing Management, 32(7), 585-594\n- **DOI**: 10.1016/S0019-8501(03)00007-5\n- **Key Findings**: Based on four case studies, identified six interrelated processes: planning, information search, selection, evaluation, choice, and negotiations. Grounded in Webster & Wind (1972) and Sheth (1973) OBB models. Revealed that the ERP acquisition process is far more complex than traditional OBB models suggest, with significant organizational readiness requirements.\n\n### 8b. Palanisamy, R., Verville, J., Bernadas, C. & Taskin, N. (2010). \"An Empirical Study on the Influences on the Acquisition of Enterprise Software Decisions\"\n- **Journal**: Journal of Enterprise Information Management, 23(5), 610-639\n- **DOI**: 10.1108/17410391011083065\n- **Key Findings**: Surveyed IS professionals involved in enterprise software acquisition. Five significant factors emerged: (1) ES strategy and performance, (2) BPR and adaptability, (3) management commitment and user buy-in, (4) single vendor integrated solution, and (5) consultants, team-location, and vendor's financing. Used OBB models as theoretical foundation; confirmed their utility for enterprise software decisions.\n\n### 8c. Loebbecke, C., Weiss, T., Powell, P. & Krooss, C. \"Drivers of B2B Software Purchase Decisions\"\n- **Source**: Frontiers in Artificial Intelligence and Applications, Vol. 212, pp. 535-546\n- **DOI**: 10.3233/978-1-60750-577-8-535\n- **Key Findings**: Developed and tested a model of B2B software purchase decisions. Two categories of drivers: **information-related** (customer references, expert network recommendations) and **feature-related** (price performance, functionality, sales team service). Both categories play major roles. Customer references and expert recommendations were particularly influential.\n\n### 8d. Anderson, J.C., Narus, J.A. & Wouters, M. (2014). \"Tiebreaker Selling\"\n- **Journal**: Harvard Business Review, March-April 2014\n- **URL**: https://hbr.org/2014/03/tiebreaker-selling\n- **Key Findings**: B2B customers are increasingly sophisticated, often treating most purchases as non-strategic. They first seek suppliers meeting basic specs at competitive price, then ask finalists to offer \"something more.\" Non-strategic suppliers can differentiate by helping customers solve problems outside the core purchase -- the \"tiebreaker\" approach.\n\n### 8e. Keranen, J., Terho, H. & Saurama, A. (2021). \"Three Ways to Sell Value in B2B Markets\"\n- **Journal**: MIT Sloan Management Review, Fall 2021\n- **URL**: https://sloanreview.mit.edu/article/three-ways-to-sell-value-in-b2b-markets/\n- **Key Findings**: Identifies three value-based selling approaches relevant to competitive deal analysis in B2B markets.\n\n---\n\n## 9. TECHNOLOGY ADOPTION & CROSSING THE CHASM\n\n### 9a. Moore, G.A. (1991/2014). \"Crossing the Chasm\" (3rd Edition)\n- **Publisher**: Harper Business\n- **Key Findings**: In the Technology Adoption Life Cycle, there is a **vast chasm between early adopters and the early majority**. Early adopters are willing to sacrifice for being first; the early majority waits until technology demonstrably improves productivity. The model is derived from original agricultural research (Rogers' diffusion of innovations) but transplanted to technology markets. Each psychographic group requires a fundamentally different marketing approach.\n\n### 9b. Crossing the Chasm Applied to Healthcare Telehealth\n- **Paper**: \"Crossing the Chasm: From Adoption to Diffusion of a Telehealth Innovation\"\n- **URL**: https://scispace.com/pdf/crossing-the-chasm-from-adoption-to-diffusion-of-a-b5ql1msnkz.pdf\n- **Key Findings**: Longitudinal empirical investigation applying Moore's chasm metaphor to telehealth. Identified a chasm between initial adoption as a prototype within a hospital network and subsequent diffusion as a commercialized product. Many telehealth initiatives fail to go beyond prototype despite being technically viable and medically relevant. The paper analyzes how key actors negotiated the chasm to successfully diffuse beyond the initial hospital setting.\n\n### 9c. Moore on Healthcare and AI (2024)\n- **Source**: Forbes interview\n- **URL**: https://www.forbes.com/sites/randybean/2024/07/14/crossing-the-ai-chasm-with-technology-thinker-and-visionary-geoffrey-moore/\n- **Key Findings**: Moore states **\"Healthcare has been struggling to adopt a value-based care model but has not had the data or the productivity tools to make it work well. AI changes that equation.\"** AI has already crossed the chasm in some use cases (high-frequency trading, digital ad placement, regulatory compliance). Next beachheads will be organizations under \"unsurmountable performance pressure\" including healthcare. The transition is from people who believe what you believe (Early Market) to people who need what you have (Beachhead Market).\n\n---\n\n## 10. JOBS TO BE DONE FRAMEWORK\n\n### 10a. Christensen, C.M., Hall, T., Dillon, K. & Duncan, D.S. (2016). \"Know Your Customers' Jobs to Be Done\"\n- **Journal**: Harvard Business Review, September 2016 (Reprint R1609D)\n- **URL**: https://hbr.org/2016/09/know-your-customers-jobs-to-be-done\n- **Full PDF**: https://www.relativimpact.com/downloads/Christensen-etal-Jobs.pdf\n- **Key Findings**: Product developers focus too much on customer profiles and data correlations. Innovation success comes from understanding the **\"job\"** a customer is trying to get done. JTBD reveals the circumstances and forces that drive people toward and away from decisions. Applied to B2B vendor selection, this means understanding the organizational progress buyers are trying to make, not just feature comparisons.\n\n### 10b. JTBD Applied to Win/Loss Analysis\n- **Source**: Arkaro B2B Consultancy\n- **URL**: https://arkaro.com/win-loss-analysis/\n- **Key Findings**: Win/loss analysis executed through JTBD methodology and external perspective bridges the innovation readiness gap. BCG (2024) found only **3% of companies** scored in the \"ready zone\" for innovation (down from 20% in 2022). **52% of innovation leaders** cite unclear strategy as primary challenge. JTBD-based win/loss provides the customer intelligence to focus innovation on what customers actually value.\n\n---\n\n## 11. B2B BUYING PROCESS: RECENT GARTNER DATA (2024-2025)\n\n### 11a. Gartner Sales Survey (2025)\n- **URL**: https://www.gartner.com/en/newsroom/press-releases/2025-06-25-gartner-sales-survey-finds-61-percent-of-b2b-buyers-prefer-a-rep-free-buying-experience\n- **Sample**: 632 B2B buyers (August-September 2024)\n- **Key Findings**:\n  - **61%** of B2B buyers prefer a rep-free buying experience\n  - **73%** actively avoid suppliers sending irrelevant outreach\n  - **69%** report inconsistencies between website info and seller info\n  - **27%** of buying time is spent researching independently online\n  - Only **17%** is spent meeting with potential suppliers\n  - Buyers spend only **5-6%** of total buying time with any single sales rep\n\n---\n\n## SUMMARY: KEY STATISTICS FOR COMPETITIVE DEAL ANALYSIS\n\n| Metric | Finding | Source of 5,000 buyers |\n| Deals involving head-to-head competition | 68% | Crayon 2025 |\n| Sales team self-reported loss reasons that are wrong | 60%+ | Corporate Visions (100K+ deals) |\n| CRM data on lost deals that is fundamentally wrong | 85% | Corporate Visions |\n| Revenue increase from comprehensive win/loss programs | 15-30% | Gartner |\n| Win rate improvement from comprehensive win/loss | up to 50% | Gartner |\n| Companies reporting win-rate increase from win/loss | 63% (84% for 3yr+ programs) | Klue 2025 |\n| Impact of combining win/loss + CI programs | 3x more likely to report
